An additional TV system is provided\nabove the antenna for monitoring deployment and the effects of dynamic inputs from a\ndifferent view. Although Reaction Control Subsystem (RCS) elements are shown, the requirement to supplement the Orbiter AVeS with RCS on the antenna structure is yet to be\ndetermined (See subsection 4.8 for further discussion) and RCS components have not been\nincluded in our sizing. A subsatellite, deployed from the Orbiter payload bay using a\nspin/tilt table, provides RF signals for pattern measurements.\n\n(See subsection 4.7.1 for\n\nsubsatellite definition).\n\ni\n\n,\n\n.~\n\nThe radiometer experiment configuration is basically the same as that for the reflector\ndevelopment flight with modifications as required to provide a multibearn off-set feed and\nradiometer electronics. These differences are shown functionally in Figure 4-45.\n\nFeed\n\noffset is provided by an additional feed extension structure (mast) approximately 33 meters\nlong which is attached to the top of original feed mast. A modification in attachment and\nposition control of the reflector surface completes the off-set paraboloid geometry with an\nF /D of L 5. The feed consists of 50 dipole subarrays (4 x 4 dipoles each) deployed using a\nwire wheel structure and arranged to provide an effective coverage swath of at least 40\nnautical miles.\n\nSignals from e::l.Ch subarray go to a low noise RF preamp before being fed\n\ninto a 50 channel receiver/integrator. Data is digitized and multiplexed prior to recording\non the orbiter. Development flight instrumentation for structure measurements is retained\nin this configuration but the RF source sUbsatellite is not required.\n\n-\n\n.\n,\n-.~\n\nThe communications experiment preliminary design has been configured as a free\nflyer to provide increased operational flexibility.\n\nThe antenna is deployed after being re-\n\n~\n\nI\n\nleased from the orbiter. Following deployment the Orbiter OMS is used to provide a\nseparation for mission operations which will include demonstration of multibeam performance\nby transmission of signals between the antenna and the Orbiter.\n\nTests between the antenna\n\nand ground stations will also be conducted (for short periods when LOS is available).\nBootlace array hardware used in the antenna development configuration can be reused\nin the commtmication experiment. The only exception is the multibeam feed consisting of\nsix dipole subarrays (2 x 2).\n\nThe feed is mounted at the end of a 65m mast to provide on\n\n\'I\n\nF/D of 0.65.\nThe bootlace array is converted to a free flying satellite by addition of subsystems in-\n\n"I\nI\n\ncluding communications, TT&C, AVCS and EPS (Figure 4-46).\n\nComponents are located in\n\nan upper systems package or lower systems package separated by the feed mast as indi-\n\ncated.\n\nSubsystems have been sized for a seven-day mission. A block\ndiagram of the system satisfying these requirements is shown in Figure 4\xc2\xb7-47. The subsatellite consists of a spin stabilized cylindrical structure with slot antennas spaced around\nthe circumference of the cylinder providing omni coverage. A transmitted power of 0.2\nw~tts will provide a 60 dB SiN at the deployable antenna feed receiver.\n\nRedundant battery/\n\ntransmitter chains are both activated prior to deployment; one into a dummy load. Switching\nbetween transmitters is controlled by a command receiver. Both command and tracking\nfunctions, using the transponder, are performed from the orbiter.\n(AgZn) are sized for a 10 watt load over a five day mission.\n\nThe primary batteries\n\nTotal payload weight, including\n\nOrbiter mounting structure and spin up/release mechanism is estimated to be 180 pounds.\nThe Teleoperator Retrieval System (TRS) was considered as an alternative for deploying the RF source (Figure 4-48).\n\nUsed for this function, the TRS serves as propulsion for\n\nestablishing the required 50 nautical miles separation as well as retrieval at the end of the\nmission.\n\nDuring the one to two day mission it provides a stable platform for the RF source,\n\nthe only interface between the two being structural (the RF source is self sufficient with\n\n.1\'\n\npower and electronics, etc.).\nTwo other potential applications of the TRS for the demonstration program have also\nbeen identified. In the first, the TRS can support visual (TV) monitoring of antenna\ndeployment/retrieval.\n\nRemote inspection of the antenna from different aspect angles can\n\nbe provided. Two operational periods are required up to two hours each. In the second\napplication the TRS can serve as a propulsion subsystem for deployment/retrieval of the\ncommunications free flying experiment.\n\nIn its four propulsion tank configuration the TRS\n\ncan provide a total Do v of over 4000 feet per second with a 6000 pound payload.\n\nThe total\n\nmission is seven days in duration.\nEvaluation of the current TRS baseline design revealed some limitations in its use\nfor these applications; the major constraint being a design life-time of 3.5 hours.\n\nEPS and\n\nAVeS performance would require modification for operation over an extended period of time.\n\n4-67\n\n~--\n\nPRIMA RY\nBATT ERY\n\nPRIMA RY\nBATT ERY\n\n--+\n\n~\n\nTRANSMITTER\n\nn..\nU\n\nTRANSMITTER\n\nSWITCH\n\n- - These results were based on a worst case orientation of a worst case\nconfiguration (the 50 cone reflector) with a greater cross-section area than any of the recommended configurations. In addition, the analysis did not take into account:\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMolecular flow properties of atmosphere\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nCross products of inertia\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThruster logic of shuttle control systems\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nStructural vibrations modes\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nUse of Res on the antenna to supplement the Orbiter AVCS by extending the\nmoment ann.\n\nWhile a detail study of aerodynamic effects including these factors is yet to be conducted for specific configurations, the general conclusion that the antenna systems should\nbe deployed at a higher altitude is still considered valid.\nrange from 7400 to 9865 pounds.\n\nThe shuttle attached payloads\n\nDeployment of these payloads above 300 nautical miles\n\nrequires the addition of one OMS kit (Figure 4-50). At the higher altitude the orbital\nmaneuver\'s requirement is less than 50 feet per second for the seven-day mission:\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nRF source deployment\n\n40 fps\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nDrag makeup\n\n10 fps\n\nThis is well within the OMS capability of a 1000 feet per second (less the amount used for\ninitial orbit insertion).\n\nl\n\nAn estimate of antenna pointing requirements is summarized in Figure 4-51. Also\nshown for comparison is the pointing accuracy available using the Orbiter IMU.\n\nPointing\n\nfor structural measurements and the radiometer experiment are within these capabilities,\nbut some augmentation with sensors mounted on the antenna structure is recommended for\nbeam pattern measurements. For spacecraft diameters in excess of 13 feet, the\nocean-going barge or ship is the most feasible way to ship.\n\nThe spacecraft could be taken\n\nby truck to a harbor by a special route, and loaded there on an ocean-going ship which would\ntake the spacecraft to the Cape Canaveral Port and from there to Kennedy Space Flight\nCenter.\n\n4. 9.2 Electrical GSE\nFor electrical GSE, a console containing two racks of equipment will be needed to\nsupport the assembly and checkout testing of the spacecraft.\n\nThe console will have displays\n\nand controls that will permit it to perform many electrical operations\n\no\n\nAmong these elec-\n\ntrical operations that the operator of the console will be able to perform are the RF tests on\nthe spacecraft receiver, and the two transmitters located in the subsatellite.\n\nThe operator\n\nwill also generate RF commands to the command receiver in the subsatellite. He will check\nthe ReS control logic and the status of the spacecraft relays.\n\nThe operator of the console\n\nwill make continuity checks on the various spacecraft drive motors and its deployment\nmechanism. In addition, he will verify the instrumentation wiring and the sensors that are\nused in the instrumentation system. In the lower portion of the console, there will be power\nsupplies that will provide the required power to the spacecraft and the free flying satellite.\n4 9 3 Fluid GSE\n0\n\nI,\n,\n\n0\n\n\'\n\nIf a requirement is established for propellant tanks in the reaction control system\n\n(RCS), fluid GSE will be provided consisting of a nitrogen checkout and servicing unit.\n\nThis\n\nunit will accept high pressure from the facility supply and regulate, control, filter and\ndeliver it to the spacecraft at the required pressure.\n\nLoading may be accomplished locally\n\nor by means of a remote electrically operated controller.\n\nThe spacecraft tank pressure and\n\ntemperature will be monitored to accomplish loading to a predetermined pressure/temperature curve.\n\nThe unit will consist of a portable, aluminum cabinet within which will be\n\n,\n\nmounted valves, regulators, relief valves and filters. An interconnecting hose set will also\nbe supplied. If the final design of the RCS system should utilize hydrazine instead of\nnitrogen as a propellant, a hydrazine servicing cart will be utilized in addition to the GN\n2\npressurization unit.\n\n4-81/4-82\n\n~\n\n~--\n\n.\n\n...\n\n...\n\nSection 5\nORBITAL TEST AND OPERA TIONS ANALYSIS (TASK 4)\nA flight test program has been developed that will demonstrate the deployable\nantennas, deployment, structural tolerance, RF pattern and limited mission capability.\nA step-by-step approach to flight planning has been taken assuming both antenna configurations, phased array and reflector, as well as a limited mission application will be\ndemonstrated on separate shuttle flights.\nTwo potential missions have been identified for limited mission testing. A radiometer mission will utilize a reflector antenna While a communications mission Will utilize\na bootlace array.\n\nMission functions for each of these missions have been defined and are\n\nincluded in this report.\nDefinition of the ground program from development test through launch operations\nwas required for the proper identification of program resources and costing and as such\nis described.\nA measurement system is required to evaluate how each antenna type meets its\nstructural accuracy reqUirements after deployment.\n\n-~\n\nThe requirements for such a system\n\nwere defined and an investigation was made to locate a system that would meet these\nrequirements.\n\n1\n\'!\n\nThis investigation found that a combination of electro-optical distance\n\nmeafJUring p.quipment and analytical photogrammetry will meet the structural tolerance\nmeasurement requirements.\n5.1 MEASUREMENT TECHNOLOGY\nThe instrumentation used to measure structural load and thermal characteristics is\ndescribed in Subsection 4. 6. This instrumentation consists of state ofthe art space qualified\n\n~.\n\n;:;train and thermo-couple gages. However, this instrumentation will not provide the data\nneeded to measure antenna structural tolerances.\nRequirements for a deployable antenna structural tolerance measurement system\nare:\n\n= . 002\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAccuracy\n\nin.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nProvide the capability to measurement a total test condition\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSpace compatable\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMinimum development.\n\n5-1\n\n\'I\n\nAn investigation of measurement systems and instruments was made to find one that\nwould satisfy these requirements.\nments.\n\nNo one system was found that can meet all the require-\n\nHowever, two systems studied if used together will meet the requirements.\n\nThese systems are described below:\n5.1. 1 Electro-Optical Distance Measuring Equipment (E-O DME)\nElectro-Optical Distance Measuring Equipment (E-O DME) measures distance by\ndetermining the transit time of modulated electromagnetic radiation from a transmitter\nto a reflector target and back to a recei"er located at the transmitter.\n\nDetermining this\n\ntransit time is done indirectly by mea.suring the phase difference between the transmitted\nmodulation and the received modulation which has traversed the double path. Since the\nvelocity of the electromagnetiC radiation carrier is known, the time of transit is proportional to the measured modulation phase shift plus\n\n~n\n\nintegral number of full phase\n\nrotations.\nCommercial instruments, that utilize the above principle, are used for engineering\nand land surveys.\n\nFigure 5-1 is a simplified block diagram of a typical instrument.\n\nThese instruments have a 3 lffil measurement range and a 1. 5 mm advertised accuracy with\nan internal instrument accuracy of 0.5 mm.\n\nGallium arsenide IR transmitting diodes are\n\nused. IR lasing diodes are used instead of visible light lasers for these commercial\ninstruments because of legal restrictions. The highest modulation frequency found in a\ncommercial instrument is 75 MHz permitting a transit time determination to an accuracy\nof approximately 10 p seconds.\nModifications are required to be made to the current commercial units to provide a\nspace compatible instrument with an accuracy of O. 1 mm.\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThese modifications are:\n\nChange the IR lasing diode to a visible light laser with the capability of\nbeing modulated at a higher than 7.5 MHz frequency\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nIncrease the modulation frequency\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nRepackage and space qualify\n\nCorner-cube-reflector targets will be located in each quadrant of the antenna.\nThree E-O DME instruments will simultaneously measure the distance from a reference\nplane to a target.\n\nBy trilateration the location of each target will be determined.\n\nreference plane will be located in the Shuttle payloads bay.\nmeasurement system.\n\nThe\n\nFigure 5-2 illustrates this\n\nSUitable target locations are selected reflector modes and array\n\nmini-hinge areas. Approximately 48 targets will be required.\n\n5-2\n\n,..,_.\n\n,",""",""~.~\',,,,",""\'"- . , . . .\'l"""""\'\'\'........~.-~\n.....\n\nI\n\n~\ni\n. ,\'o.\n\nj\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 MODI FIED COMM ERCIA L INSTRUMENT\nMOD\nOSC\n\n1--------oeI\nREFERENCE\n\nTx\n\nDIODE\nTARG ET\n\nDISPL AY\n\nPHASE\nMEAS\n\nAMP\n\nRx\nDIODE\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 CURR ENT INSTR UMEN T (TYPICAL)\n- 3 km RANGE\n- 1.5 mm ADVE RTISE D ACCURACY\n- 0.5 mm CAPA BILITY\n- 75 MHz MOD FREQ\n- TEN YEAR OLD TECHNOLOGY\n\xe2\x80\xa2 MODI FICATIONS\n- CHANGE DIODE\n- INCREASE MOD FREQ\n- REPACKAGE\n- SPACE QUAL\n1416-17 1 V\n\n,I.\n\nfl\n\n\'i\nFigure 5-1 Structu ral Tolerance Measurement With Electro-Optical Distanc\n(\' Me!isurement Equipment\n\n5-3\n\nr--~\n\nI~-""""\n\n1\n\n~\n\nr\n\n1\n\nI\n\n.,\n\n~\n\n1\n\n..".\n\nANTENNA\n\nCf\nfo!::.\n\nE-O DME\nRANGEFINDERS\n\nE-O DME\nRANGEFINDERS (3)\n1416-172V\n\nl\n\nFigure 5-2 E-O OME Test.Configuration\n\nj\n~I\n\n[\nt\n\n. J\n\n\'"\n\n.....\n\n\'..\n\n.,.\n\n.~~\n\n-\n\n.........\n\n---\n\n....\n\n.\n\n,.\n\n~~~,\n\n........,..,.-.......- _ . _ .::o:\'l:,=..\n\n~~~---,,--\n\nj\n\n."\n\nj\n\nTo achieve the required accuracy it will be necessary to obtain continuous measurement data at a particular target for a period of time and to statistically reduce these data\nto determine the target location. The amount of time required at each target is dependent\non the natural frequency of the structure to which the target is attached. It is estimated\nthat it will take approximately one orbit to determine the location of all the targets. Therefore, it will not be possible to maintain a single test condition while all target locations\nare determined. A photogrammetric measurement technique, while not having the accuracy\nof the E-O DME technique, will however, determine the instantaneous surface conformity\nof the total antenna su rface .\n5. 1. 2 Photogrammetry Surface Conformity\nAnalytical photogrammetry is a process whereby data from two or more photographs\nof a structure are analyzed to produce a contour map of the structure.\n\nSince this process\n\nis a mathematical one, accurate locations of the camera stations are not necessary.\n\nThe\n\ndata, in the form of exposed photographic plates are reduced in the laboratory to produce\ncontour maps of the total surface. Accuracies of one part in 120,000 of an anienna\ndiameter have been obtained using this method under optimum conditions.\nA three-camera photogrammetri.c net will be used for all Shuttle attached structural\ndemonstration missions.\n\nOne camera will be located in the payload bay and the other two\n\nwill be positioned and supported by the remote manipulators. Approximately 250 simple\npaint-on or self-adhesive targets will be required to define the antenna\'s surface.\n\nThese\n\ntargets will be applied during antenna fabrication.\nFigure 5-3 illustrates a typical antenna surface conformity contour map that was\nconstructed from photogrammetric data.\n\nThe antenna is a 300 foot National Radio\n\nAstronomy Observatory (NRAO) antenna at Greenbank, West Virginia. That the photographs\n\n.\'\n\nfor this analysis were primarily taken from helicopter verifies the non-surveyed\n\n,\n\n,:\n\ncamera site approach.\n\n"\n\n5.2 GROUND TEST\n\nGround testing is divided into two major parts as illustrated in Figure 5-4. A tabulation of the\ndata that was required but not available was made and this tabulation became the requirements for development tests.\n\nFigure 5-5 is a matrix correlating these development test\n\nrequirements with the specific tests (Figure 5-4) that have been planned to satisfy these\nrequirements.\nThe following paragraphs present a brief description of each of the planned development tests.\nRim Hinge Test - This spring mechanism provides the stored energy for release and\nis the prime mover for deploying the wire-wheel antenna assembly.\n\nReliable operation of\n\nthis mechanism and an understanding of its torque, friction, and locking characteristics\nare critical to the design.\nStub ends of representative rim elements will be fitted to the rim hinge test specimen.\nOne end will be fixed and the other attached to a resisting load such as a hydraulic device\nor a system of weights. The entire assembly will be capable of being orien.ted with respect\nto the vertical. Instrumentation will consist of standard strain gages, load-links, angular\nand linear displacement meters, and thermocouples for the thermal tests planned.\nAt various positions during hinge motion, rotation, static and breakaway measurements will be taken and recorded using automated means.\n\nThese measurements will be\n\nrepeated at a number of fixture orientations.\nRim Member Characteristics - The thermal and structural characteristics of a\ngraphite/epoxy composite column have to be know:l to properly design the rim members.\nThis data is required for both phase array and l\'eflector designs. The moment required to rotate the tube through definite angles will be\nmeasured, and the modulus calculated.\nThe modulus of elasticity wUl be determined by measuring deflection witbin center\nof the simply supported column. The column will be mounted vertically to negate gravity\neffects.\nThe Euler column load can be approximated by plotting lateral deflections versus\naxial load which have been applied with known eccentricity. A family of curves will be\nproduced, one curve for each eccentricity which will approach the critical Euler column\nload asymptotically.\nTo meet reflector tolerance requirements it is essential to know column deflection\ncharacteristics due to expected gore loads. Rim hinge fittings will be attached to the\ncolumn ends and the column wUl be supported at these fittings.\n\nGore attachment fittings\n\nwill be installed on the column and loads will be applied through these fittings.\n\nLateral\n\nand torsional deflections all along the column will be measured.\nThe thermal coefficient of expansion for this type of column will be determined by\nuniformly heating the column and measuring the column\'s expansion with an interferometer.\nStay Static and Temperature Coefficient Test - Theoretical structural and thermal\ncharacteristics data are available and test data of small samples has been taken.\n\nHow-\n\never, no test data is available for the unique design of the antenna stays and tension links.\nTests will be performed to determine the following:\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nStatic load strength\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nElongation under load\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThermal coefficient of expansion.\n\nFull length stays will be incrementally loaded in a horizontal position. Load and\nelongation data will be taken.\nStay thermal coefficient of expansion will be determined by applying a low pretenSion\nload to the stay and heating the stay in steps. Length changes will be measured at each\ntemperature step. Due to the length of a stay (excess of 200 foot) the test will be done\n,\n\nincremently. A chamber approximately 50 foot long will be used.\n\nt\'\n\n5-10\n,\ni\n\n~:\n\n.L... __~____\n...\xc2\xb7,"_\'_,,\n~;.~\n\n:::..=o-.e\'-\'\'\'\'\'-\'"\'\'iL\n\nThe stay will be on\n\n\'\n\n\'-\',,,\\),\'\n\n-\n\n-\n\n,\n\nr\n\nreels and will be stretched through the test chamber along a teflon covered table. Each\n\nI,\n\nstay segment will be tested to determine the coefficient of expansion and to verify thermal\ncharacteristics uniformly over the entire length of the stay.\nGore Wrap/Unwrap Test - Demonstration that flight-type gore structure can be\nreliably deployed from its drum is critical to the wire wheel concept.\nTheproposed test consists of fabricating a number of prototype gore segments (mini-\n\n.\n.\n\nmum ofthree), and repeatedly deploying them from their storage drum, while measuring\n\n..1\n\nforces and deflections experienced by one assembly. Each gore will be fabricated to\nrepresent the inboard section of an actual gore in order to account for realistic drum\nattachment.\n\nf\n\nI\n\n,\n\ni\n\nThree identical gore assemblies are attached at their vertices to the primary wrapdrum; the drum would be a reduced-size version of the flight drum. One of the three gores\nwill be instrumented and deployed vertically to avoid out-of-plane deflection due to gravity.\nDeployment is accomplished through the use of a torque motor and tension tape assembly\nthat acts on a gore spreader.\n\nThe spreader represents a rim member and will be rotated\n\nto simulate the gore twisting that would occur in actual deployment.\n\nThe other two gores\n\nare wrapped on auxiliary drums as they roll from the deployment drum. Wrapping is\nI\n;\n\n~.\n\nl ~\n\n, I\n\n,i\n\n, I\n\naccomplished in reverse with torque motors driving both the primary wrap drum and the\nauxiliary drums and the gore spreader lowered to accurately reproduce actual wrapping.\nMeasurements will be made of deployment and wrap rates as well as induced strains\nand forces. This information will be useful in test/analysis correlation work.\n\nMotion\n\n,\n\npicture photography will be used to observe gore element behavior, including edge clip\n\nI\n\ndisengagement from the drum studs. If a backup measurement technique is used (to the\nsurrogate payload electronics) to determine antenna in-plane tolerances, consideration\nwill be given to demonstrating the technique during this test programo\n\nThe accuracy of\n\nthe measurements can be easily verified and/or calibrated using standard operating\ntechniqueso\n\n..-\n\nStay Reel/Mounting Hing Test - This mechanism provides the alignment and drag\nforces necessary to control the deployment rate of the wire wheel rim assembly and gore\npanels.\nment.\n\nReliability of this system must be established to assure proper antenna deployThis test will measure the torque and friction forces generated by the stay reel\n\nassembly and provide data to finalize the design of the reels and torque motors.\n\n5-11\n\n~\n\nJ\n\n;1\n\n;1\n\n/1\n\nI\n\n\'I\n\n\'~\'\\~\n\n- -\n\nj\n\n,"\n\n~\n\nPrototype stay-reels will be mounted on a mounting ring attached to structure simulating the antenna drum. A test fixture consisting of three degree of freedom cars on a track\nwill simulate rim-hinge travel.\n\nstay-reel stays will be attached to the fixture cars. With\n\na uniform load applied to each stay, the static and breakaway torque measurements will be\ntaken. At various stages of deployment, measurements will be taken to determine the stay\ntension (torque) required to maintain a uniform rate of deployment.\n\nThe minimum angle\n\nbetween stays and rim Will be established to prevent stay overloading. As the stays are\npaid out, the drum section will be rotated and the rim to stay reel angle will change to\nbimulate gore panel deployment.\n\nFriction and torque (static and breakaway) measurements\n\nwill be taken between the drum and mounting ring. During deployment of the stays from\ntheir reels, a continuous check will be made to determine the rim/stay-reel tracking\ncapability\n\n0\n\nGore Segment Thermal Test - Tests are planned to demonstrate that the thermal\ncharacteristics of the complex gore assembly are compatible with the operational\nrequirements of space.\nThe complex mechanical configuration of the three-layer gore assembly requires a\nground test to establish thermal characteristics and to validate the thermal model of the\ngore. Temperature operating levels and gradients Within the gore must be established.\nMechanical measurements will be made to verify thermal deflection predictions. The open\nor free area of the gore at various sun angles will be established.\n\nThese data are necessary\n\nto refine the structural element orbit heat flux environment calculations.\nA segment of the gore measuring approximately 3 ft x 4 ft and containing all the\nnecessary prototype structural, mechanical, and thermal components will be provided for\nthe gore thermal vacuum test. Depending upon gore fabrication techniques, the test article\ncould be a segment of one of the gores built for the gore wrap/unwrap test described above.\nThe test article, installed in a supporting fixture providing the necessary instrumentation\nto measure forces and deflections, will be installed in a solar Simulation test facility.\nGore temperatures will be recorded at various sun angles of incidence representing\nexpected flight conditions.\nBootlace Lens Pattern Measurements - A bootlace array will consist of tens ofthousands\nof nominally identical subarrays. Attainment of the predicted antenna characteristics will,\ntherefore, be strongly dependent upon the repeatability of the manufacturing process which\nproduces the subarrays. One objective of \'this series of tests is to measure the characteristics of representative numbers of subarrays and the resulting overall array characteristics, so that statistical tolerances can be established on deviations during production.\n\n5-12\n\n!\n!\n!-\n\n.1\n\nl\n\n....\n\nThe statistical measurement program will require building a minimum of 200 subarrays whose characteristics will he evaluated.\n\nPattern gain, beamwidth, sidelobes, and\n\nimpedance data will be taken on the subarrays and reduced to a statistical format.\n\nThe data\n\nwill be used to calculate overall performance of the antenna and to predict performance of\nt,he full array of subarrays.\n\nThe test antenna will comprise\'two antenna planes\', each consisting of 100 subarrays\narranged in a 10 x 10 matrix.\n\nTrombone phase shifters and a movable feed will be used to\n\nscan the beam.\nPattern measurements will consist of generating sufficient pattern information to\nevaluate the subarrays and allow prOjection to space-based array performance. Subarray\ntests will consist of measuring gain, sidelobe levels, be amwidth, cross-polarized patterns,\nand impedance measurements both in-band and out-of-band as required.\nRadiometer Reflector Feed Pattern - The radiometer offset reflector feed determines\nthe antenna\'s ground footprint.\nindividual spots i.n\n\nThis footprint, a staggered pushbroom, provides 50\n\naconfigur~tion\n\nto the direction of antenna travel.\n\nthat results in a continuous ground swath perpendicular\nTherefore a large area is covered with the capability to\n\ndifferentiate local data within this area.\nReflector feed pattern tests will be performed to determine feed performance and\npattern in order to verify overall radiometer antenna performance. Scale model pattern\ntest techniques will be used to obtain a measure of the feed pattern including all sidelobes.\nDeployment Computer Model - Deployment computer models of the array and reflector\nconfigurations are required to support and verify all phases of antenna designs.\nI\'\n\nPreliminary\n\nanalysis resulting from an initial model will define the requirements for structural and\nmechanical components. For example, the rim hinge springs will be sized for adequate\ntorque throughout deployment.\n\nAlso, acceptable stay and gore payout rates will be\n\ndetermined and the payout motors and gear assemblies will be defined.\n\nThis initial model\n\nassumes a perfect deployment with rigid members and involves kinetic and kinematic\nsimulations. Besides defining initial design requirements the computer model will be used\nI\n\nto determine the sequences and the time for a controlled deployment.\nAs the design and the program matures the deployment computer model will be updated\n\nand expanded to include the actual hardware parameters determined during component\ndevelopment testing.\n\nThis updating will permit comprehensive analysis, including structural\n\nflexibility, of the structure and deployment.\n\nSince deployment of both reflector and array\n\n5-13\n\n, ""\n\n~,\n\n___ ,: C-", :, :!.."\'-\'\n\n.".~\n\nI\n- ,",;;:,\n\n.....\n\n--ill\n\n,.\n\nantenn as are design ed to occur at an extrem ely slow rate, the accele\nration forces will be\nneglig ible. Prime member support with a simple "peter pan" rig will permit partial antenna\ndeployment.\nFunctional deployment testing to verify the antenna after assembly and before and\n\n!.\n\nafter environmental tests is impracticable. This type of test would require an extremely\n\n! ,;\n\nf .\n\nlarge and costly test fixture to deploy the entire antenna without damage to the structure of\ngores.\n\nAfter such a test deployment there would be no guarantee that damage would not\n\noccur during rewrapping. After studying this problem the following test method has been\nchosen to verify antenna assembly and environmental survival.\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPartial deployment to verify electrical and mechanical deployment functions\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nEnd-to-end verification of instrumentation channels and other electrical circuits\nusing support equipment stimuli and monitoring equipment\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nDisplacement gages that will sense out of specification movement of buried antenna\ncomponents such as gore to drum attachment.\n\nAfter antenna assembly and prior to the start of structural and environmental prototype testing the antenna will be partially deployed in the main assembly and test fixture. On\nboard instrumentation data will be checked and visual observation made to verify antenna\nfunction. The displacement gages will be checked to verify that any movement is within\nspecification.. Upon restoring the antenna a check will be made of the integrity of all the\ninstrumentation circuits. This test will be made before each environmental test.\nStatic Test - This test will verify the antenna ability to survive the steady state launch\nacceleration forces.\nThe simplest method of testing a structure like a wire wheel antenna would be to use a\ncentrifuge. However, the antenna length in the launch configuration makes a centrifuge test\nimpracticable. A centrifuge with an arm in excess of 350 foot would be needed to produce\na force gradient of less than 10 percent across the packaged antenna.\n\nSuch a machine is not\n\navailable. Therefore, static loading methods will be used. The major difficulty with this\n\n,\n\n\'I\n\nmethod is applying uniform loads to the gores. For the bootlace array configuration the\nloads will be applied at the gore hinges and for the reflector loads will be applied at the\nnodes.\n\nI\n~.!\n\n1\n\nstrain and deflection measurements will be taken during the test. At the completion\nof the test the antenna will be partially deployed and inspected for damage.\n\n5-15\n\n~-\n\n.,\n\n-\n\n-.-.,\n\n,\n\n.......-...~~,... . . ._ . _ _\xe2\x80\xa2" " .\n...\n, \xe2\x80\xa2 1\n~-----...,_\n\nI\n\n,\'"\n\n1\n\nAcoustic Vibration - The test to verify the antenna\'s ability to survive launch vibration\n\nwill be performed in an acoustic chamber. The antenna will be held in the Shuttle mounting\nfixture in the launch configuration. Accelerometers located on the rurtema will monitor the\nantennas response to the acoustic energy. At the completioll of the test the antenna will be\npartially deployed and inspected for damage.\nLaunch Site Operations - At the launch site after an initial checkout period the antenna\nwill be compatibility tested with the Shuttle. All mechanical and electrical interfaces will\nbe verified. The special instrumentation, photogrametric cameras and electronic distance\nmeasuring eqUipment will be Shuttle compatibility tested as well as the antenna pattern signal\ngenerating satellite. After compatibility testing this equipment will be physically mated with\nthe Shuttle. The location of the three electronic distance measuring units on rails fixed to\neither side of the payload bay will be accurately measured and referenced to a tooling mark\nlocated on the antenna/Shuttle attachment fixture.\nOnce the antenna is installed in the Shuttle payload bay and all interfaces verified it\n\nwill be passive for the remaining launch operation.\nRefurbishment - Three missions options have been selected for the flight demonstration\nprogram:\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Shuttle attached bootlace lens\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Shuttle attached reflector\n\xe2\x80\xa2 Limited r\xc2\xb7Iission Application (Shuttle-attached reflector radiometer or free-flying\nbootlace lens communication system).\nThe approach is to use the same basic structure for all three options. Therefore\nrefurbishment and configuration change between flights is required, if more than one option\nis demonstrated.\nFor example, at the completion of a bootlace lens development flight the antenna will\nbe removed from the Shuttle and installed in the main assembly and test fixture where it will\nb~\n\ndeployed. The bootlace lens gores and the Astromasts will be removed. The Astromasts\n\nwill be fully deployed and inspected. An inspection of the wire wheel structure and mechanisms will be made. All limited life items will be replaced as will any parts showing signs\nof wear.\nAfter a complete inspection and parts refurbishment, as required, the Astromasts\nwill be reinstalled and a reflector-gore assembly will be installed. Since we now have a\nnew configuration with different structural loading than the previous bootlace lens mission,\nthe static, acoustic and acceptance vibration tests will have to be repeated.\n5-16\n\nI\n\n..\n\n~~-~~~~~ :------\'~-\n\n~\n\n1\n\nIj\ni\n"\n\n:1\n)\n\nLaunch operations for a reflector development will be the same as described for the\nbootlace lens antenna.\n5.3 FLIGHT DEMONSTRATION\nA three-mission flight program has been identified to demonstrate a flat bootlace array\nantenna, a parabolic reflector antenna and a limited mission usage communications or\nradiometer. The first two missions and the limited mission radiometer will be Shuttle\nattached flights. Power, data handling, attitude control and other spacecraft functions will\nbe provided by the Shuttle for these missions. The limited mission communications will be\na free-flyer and therefore, spacecraft subsystems will have to be added for this mission.\nFor all missions the antenna will be retrievable and capable of being brought back to earth\n\n,I\n\nat the conclusion of the flight. ""\'.,_,\n...\n\n5.3.2 Radiometer Limited Mission Demonstration - Shuttle Attached\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n.,\n\nThis mission will demonstrate a 100 m aperture deployable antenna used as a radiometer. An offset feed with low noise receivers is added to the refurbished reflector structural\ndemonstration antenna for this mission. Launch-boost, orbital checkout, and antenna\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\ndeployment operations are the same as described in 5. 3. 1 for the orbital development\ndemonstration mission. After deployment the Shuttle orients the antenna to collect earth\nradiometric data. This experiment period is during days three, four and five as illustrated\nin the Figure 5-8 Orbital Test Timeline. At the completion of the experiment period on day\nfive the antenna is retracted on day six and preparation is made for deorbit and landing on\nday seven.\n5.3.3 Multibeam Communications Limited Mission Demonstration - Free Flyer\n~e\n\nmultibeam communication mission demonstration multibeam communication per-\n\nformance of a 100 m deployable passive-bootlace-Iens antenna. Since this is a free flying\nspacecraft, subsystems and multibeam communication equipment are added to the refurbished bootlace-Iens structural demonstration antenna. Launch-boost, orbital checkout,\nand antenna deployment operations are the same as described in 5.3.1 for the orbital\ndevelopment missions. After antenna deployment and checkout the antenna with spacecraft subsystems is separated from the Shuttle. The Shuttle then establishes an orbital\nseparation distance from the antenna that permits multibeam communication between the\nShuttle and the antenna. On days three, four and five, as illustrated in the Figure 5-8,\nmission operations include demonstration of multibeam performance by transmission of\nsignals between the antenna and the Shuttle and also, for short periods when line-of-sight\nis available, between the antenna and ground station.\n\nI\n\n5-25/5-26\n~.:.:.\n\n..... ~\n\n;..,\n\nSectio n 6\nPROGRAMMATICS ANALYSIS (TASK 5)\nPrelim inary design result s from Task 3 and the demon stratio\nn flight progr am define d\nin Task 4 form the basis for progr amma tic data develo ped in\nTask 5. This program consists of a series of mission options that, for program planning purposes, have\nbeen combined into a single program.\n\nThe mission options are:\n\nOption 1 - Shuttle attached deployment and structural demonstration of a passive\nbootlace lens.\nOption 2 - Shuttle attached deployment and structural demonstration of a reflector.\ndption3A- Shuttle attached reflector radiometer mission demonstration\nOption 3B- Free-flying pa.ssive bootlace lens multibeam communications mission.\nThe program schedule is illustrated in Figure 6-2. With a program start in\nJanuary 1.980, the first demonstration antenna will be launched in January 1983. Options\n2 and 3 can be launched on one-year centers. A seven-day flight period has been planned\nfor each mission.\n\nOptions 2 and 3 are shown as additions to Option 1. Option 3A and 3B\n\nhave been scheduled in parallel with each other assuming that only a reflector radiometer\nor an array multibeam communications mission will be flown as part of the total demonstration program. All of the development required for the basic structure is included in\nOption 1 and only the development unique to a particular options configuration or mission\nis shown for that option.\nFor the three mission programs shown in Figure 6-2, one Preliminary Design\nReview (PDR) will be held While a separate Critical Design Review (CDR) will be held for\neach mission.\nAntenna pattern measJ.rements will be made as part of Option 1 and Option 2 missions.\nAn RF generating subsatellite will be required for these measurements.\n\nDeSign, fabrica-\n\n,i\n\ntion and testing of this subsatellite is included in the Option 1 activities. The subsatellite\nwill be retrieved during the Option 1 mission and refurbished for the Option 2 mission.\nOne basic wire wheel structure will be fabricated for the total program. This structure will be used for qualification test and refurbished as required for each mission.\nSince the same structure will be used for both qualification test and flight, a protoflight\ntest approach will be taken.\n\nI\n\nProtoflight testing environmentally tests at qualification\n\nlevels for acceptance duration.\n\nBetween each mission the antenna structure will be\n\ncompletely disassembled, inspected and reassembled into the next mission configuration.\nNew components will be added as required for the next mission.\n\n6-4\nI\n\nI\n\n..\n\n"--\'\n\n----\n\n--\n\n-~-- -.~-------.----.-~-.\n\nI\n...\n...\n\'"\n\'r\'\n\n1979\n\n....\n..\n\nI\n\nPDR\n\n(oj\n\n+\n\n<\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPROGRAM\n\nOPTION 2\n\nOPTION 1\n\nOPTION 3A\n\n.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2 \xe2\x80\xa2\n\nI.\nLAUNCH\n\nI\xe2\x80\xa2\nLAUNCH\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2I \xe2\x80\xa2\n\nCDR\n\nCDR\n\nLAUNCH\nOPTION 3A\n\nOPTION 2\n\nOPTION 3A OPTION 3\n\nOPTION 1 OPTION Z\n\n.\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nCDR\n\nSTART\n\n00\n\n"1l\'aa\n\n."e\n0%\n\no!:\n;0\nI\n\n.\n\n.0-0\n\nI\n\n~""""""~"""""""""""""~"PROGRAMMANAGEMENT\nI\n\n.,\n\n.~\n\nl\n\n~\n\n.i\n\n!\n\nIe::\n,),3\n\nOPTION 3\n\n+\n\nLAUNcH\n\nSTART\n\nI\n\n1985\n\n1984\n\nOPTION 3\n\n,+\n\n+\n\n+\n\nSTART\n\nCDR\n\nSTART\n\n1983\n\n1982\n\n1981\n\n1980\n\n*\'F\'f~\n\nc~\n\nI\n\nI\n-\n\n~\'"\n...\n\n. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .~_ SYSTEM ENGINEERING\n\n-\n\n. . . . . . . . . . . The first category, SR&T, For Antenna Flight\nTest, includes items that are planned to be accomplished during development of the deployable antenna for shuttle-attached flight test.\n\nThese items are design verification tests in\n\nadvance of the manufacturing phase of the flight article. The second category\xe2\x80\xa2.SR&T For\nLimited Mission Demonstration, describes design verification tests planned to precede the\nmanufacturing phase of the flight article.\n\nThe third ca.tegory, SR&T For User Applications,\n\ncovers items that will be needed following the deployable antenna flight test program.\n7.1 SR&T FOR ANTENNA FLIGHT TEST\n\nAn early start on any, or all, of the following would reduce schedule risk on the\nflight test program. Items 1 through 7 are described in 5.2.1. Items 8 through 12 are\nbriefly described here.\n1. RF pattem test of lOO-element bootlace array\n2. Gore wrap test and thermal test\n3. Rim hinge test\n4. Rim member test\n5. stay test\n6. Stay reel te st\n\n.,\n\n7. Deployment computer model\n8. Array feed pattern test - It is planned for the space-fed bootlace array to use a\nlight weight feed system similar to a single subarray of the main array. Because\nthe main array is passive, the feed array pattern enters strongly into the\nillumination taper of the main array; the feed array pattern should be known in\nadvance of design of the resistive attenuators that will be used in the main array\nfor illumination taper.\n\n7-J\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2..:... _\n\n, \':I\'\n\n.,.\n\n.,\n\n~!\n\n9. Pattern test of slot array for subsatellite - The subsatellite that will generate\nan RF signal for on ...orbit pattern tests will be a spinning body with slot radiators.\nAn RF simulation of the slot antenna system and its feed harness will verify the\ndt~sign.\n\n10. Parallel-Strips RF transmission line attenuation measurements - The main array\nuses bootlace lines to phase the elements.\n\nThese lines are parallel conducting\n\nstrips on thin Kapton, 0.25 mil copper on one mil Kapton.\nabout 0.2 dB per wavelength.\n\nThe attenuation is\n\nThe electrical length of the bootlace lines is\n\nlongest at the center of the array, but the RF illumination taper requires least\npower density at the outside of the array.\n\nTherefore, for the flight experiment,\n\nattenuators will be used in the bootlace lines to provide the desired illumination\ntaper for low sidelobes. Laboratory measurements of the propagation velocity,\nlosses, repeatability and added attenuators, are required.\n\n1\nI\n\nj\n\n\'1\n\n11. Instrumentation sensors - A design verification test of representative samples of\nthe accelerometers, thermocouples, strain gages! etc., that will be used in\nflight, with coupons of the structure, will provide data for the TT&C signal conditioning system design.\n12. Electro-optical distance measuring system - An existing design of a surveying\ninstrument will be used for precise measurements in flight, but the instrument\nwill be repackaged to separate the indicator from the optical system, and for\nspace application.\n\nThe first step should be an environmental test of the re-\n\npackaged system.\n7.2 SR&T FOR LIMITED MISSION DEMONSTRATION\nFor the radiomete r flight test, or for a communication antenn&. test, the reflector\nor lens antenna will be used.\n\nThus the items in 7.1 apply, as do the additional items\n\nlisted below.\n1. Radiometer reflector feed pattern test - This item is described in 5.2.1.\n\n2. Test of available low noise preamplifier - A cluster of feeds will be used with\nthe offset paraboloid, in the recommended configura.tion of a radiometer.\n\nEach\n\nfeed will have an associated low noise receiving preamplifier. An engineering\ntest of these preamplifiers is recommended, under thermal vacuum conditions,\nusing off-the-shelf hardware that would be the flight hardware if the test results\nare satisfactory.\n\n7-2\n\n- , . - _ . . ". . . _ _ _\n,.\n\nm\n\n3. Stripline RF filter design and test - To avoid receiver problems from man-made\nsignals, each radiometer receiving charmel should contain an RF bandpass filter,\n\n"\'I\n,I\n\nimmediately following the low noise RF preamplifier at the antenna feed. A stripline filter for this purpose, using an alumina substrate and 50-ohm characteristic impedance, should be designed and verified.\n7.3 SR&T FOR USER APPLICATIONS\nThe items listed below are not required for any of the flight test options, but are\ndevelopments to support future systems that will use large deployable antennas.\nfour items are submodules of phased array modules.\n\nThe first\n\nSome or all of them would become\n\npart, as appropriate, of phased array modules that would be manufactured for user systems.\n\n1. sAw (surface acoustic wave) bandpass filter submodule-SAW Technology is now\navailable to produce very small filters operating at radio frequencies up to 1 or\n2 GHz.\n\nLow-loss bandpass filters with high out-of-band rejection (60 dB) can\nand should be developed, of small size (1 cm2 ) for use in communication system\'\nRF modules.\n\n2. GaAs (gallium arsenide) FET (field effect transistor) amplifiers - 200-mi11iwatt\namplifier submodules will be required at the several radio frequencies (0.6, 1,\n1. 4, 4, 6, 11, and 14 GHz) that will be used by communication satellites with\n\ndeployable antennas.\n\nThese submodules should be designed for low-cost high-\n\nquantity production.\n3. Low-loss 3-bit sWitchable phase shifters - For attitude control by electronic\nbeam steering, phase shifters are needed.\n\nThe use of electronic attitude control\n\nwill reduce satellite or antenna platform cost by alleviating the requirements for\nlow-temperature coefficient structure, structure stiffness, and accurate\n\nRcsiACS\n\nsystems. The phase shifters should need low switching power and should have\nRF loss lower than 0.5 dB per bit with RF power handling capability of 200 milliwatts.\n\nThe phase shifters should be built as submodules, desigOE:ld for low-cost\n\nhigh-quantity production.\n4.\n\nRF module development - An overall RF module development program will be\nnecessary preliminary to the use of active phased array antennas for space\napplications. A module program is defined in Reference 32.\n\nThe key parameters\n\nof the RF module for any application are light weight (0.01 lb), low cost ($20.00),\nand reliability in the space environment, all of which are achievable.\n\n7-3\n\nI\n\n\'\\,\n\n5. Low noise RF preamplifiers f0r phased array radiometer - An early limited\napplication flight demonstration of a phased array radiometer is not recommended\nbecause the necessary preamplifiers are not available. The phased array antenna\nconfiguration is better than the offset parabola; it will have more gain, smaller\nspot size, lower sidelobes, and better noise figure. Unlike phased array\ncommunication or radar systems, the RF modules for the phased array radiometer\nare single chips containing only low-noise preamplifiers. The number of preamplifiers used in the array will vary from ten thousand to several million,\ndepending on the application. Except that they are smaller and less complex,\nthe radiometer RF modules will have similar requirements to the radar and\ncommunication RF modules.\n\n7-4\n\n.....\n\n)IIJ\n\n----.--~\n\n"\n\n--,\n\n... .....\n\n\'~."""-~\'~\'~\'\'\'\'\'.\'\'-\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\n\n\'j\n,,;.\n\n...\n\n,\n\nSection 8\nBffiLIOGRAPHY\n1.\n2.\n\nSpace Based Radar Vol. III Technical, 5-20-77, Report No. SAMSO TR-77-78 (Secret)\n\n3.\n\nLong Range Sensor Study, Final Technical Report, 3-15-77, BMDATC (Secret)\n\n4.\n\nOrbit Construction Demonstration Study Report NSS-OC-RP003, 5-4-76, GAC\n\n5.\n\nGAC-NASA/MSFC visit to NASA LaRC on August 23, 1977\n\n6.\n\n~\n\nJPL, Programmatic Considerations for Large Space Antenna, 15 June 1977\n\nSolar Power Satellite, Concept Evaluation, Vol. 2 Detailed Report, July 1977, NASA\n\nf\'\nI\n\nJSC 12973\n7.\n\nPublic Service Communications Satellite (PSCS) Users\' Brochure, NASA GSFC,\nMay 1977\n\n8.\n\nA New Concept for Communications Satellites - "The Switchboard in the Sky", NASA\nHeadquarters, S. Radial Tolerance\n\n0\n\nFigure C-2 Lens Tolerance (45 Phase Error Criterion)\n\nC-6\n\n.J\n\n...\n\n...,...\n\n2\'\n\nAppendix D\nDETERMINISTIC TOLERANCE ANALYSIS (EXACT)\nFOR A LENS/PHASED ARRA Y\n\nl\n\nI\n\nI\nI\n\nt\n\nl~\n\n....\n\n...\n\n-.~.\'-" ~"~\'\'\'\'-\'\'\'\'\'\'\'~\'\'\'-\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'11\'\'\'\n\n,\n\n.~\n\n,\n\ni\n!\n\nI\n\n7\n\n,J\n\nr\n[,\n\n!\n\n.......\n\nD-l\n\nAppendix D\nDETERMINISTIC TOLERANCE ANALYSIS (EXACT) FOR\nLENS/PHASED ARRA Y\n1.0 PHASED ARRA Y\n\nPermissible structural deviations are based upon antenna performance characteristics, such as sidelobe levels and beam steering errors. This appendix derives the\nrelationship between the electrical phase error and the structural deviation which causes\nthat error. The deviation is a function of the position of the element in the array, the\narray geometry and the radar beam pointing direction.\nFigure D-1 indicates the coordinate system used for the analysis. Off axis\n\nfeed locations will modify the derivations since they will further influence the lens\naberration.\nThus the following expressions result for the Simple on-axis feed location.\n\xe2\x82\xac\n\nE\n\n_ UA\na - 211"\n\nr\n\nUA\n= - 11 +\n211"\n\n1\n1\n\nJl-\n\n1+\n\nci/4?\n\n4~ /a 2\n\n"\n;.\n\n"\n:\n,I\n\nD-10\n\n~----~- w!j_sa~_ _\n...\n""\'.""-""\'""\'\'\'\'"0\'\'f?\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'\'-"\n\nr\n""",~~-",~,,,,,\n\nfsJ_u...,\xc2\xb7........__. .\n\n_II \'.....\n....\n\n~1\n\n~\n~\n\nAppendix E\nSTATISTICAL TOLERANCE ANALYSIS FOR A\nLENS/PHASED ARRA Y\n\n1\n\nE-l\n\n\xc2\xb7\xc2\xb7-~~iH"\',l"\n\n~---\n\n...\n\n4*\n\n1\n~\n\n..------------.-\n\n~\n\n.. -....~....... \'~"" ...\n\ni~,\n~\n\nI\nj\n\nI\'\n\nAppendix E\n\nf\n\nTOLERANCE ANALYSIS OF LENS\n\n1.0 SUMMARY\nJ.\n\nThe antenna pattern of an array is dete:cmined by the far field summation of the amplitude and phase of element currents in the aperture. The actual antenna pattern will differ\nfrom the theoretical pattern as a result of systematic and random errors in the currents and\nin the element positions.\nSystematic errors can be caused, for example, by the presence of a radome, by line\nattenuation that was not properly accounted for, or by mutual couplillg. Systematic errors\ncan ultimately be corrected for.\nRandom errors are those that arise in the construction or adjustments of an array,\ndue to limitations of accuracy in fabrication or measurement. These errors result in a\nrise of sidelobe levels above design values, reduction in gain and change the pointing direction of the main team. For low sidelobe antenna designs the effect on sidelobe level is the\nmost signiacant.\nThe following derivation of an error expression considers only Gaussian distributed\n\n!\n\n:\n\nand correlated errors. An equation for the normalized far field pattern is derived which has\ntwo terms. The first term is equal to the no-error pattern and the second term defines the\nsidelobe level due to random errors.\nParametric trade-off curves are generated using the derived equations. The curves\nplot mechanical radial tolerance of element location versus element correlation distance,\nangle-off boresight and random error sidelobe level.\nThe curves show, for a 304.8 meter (1000 foot) diameter space-fed kns with 813,000\nmodules, that the worst case correlation distance is 15.5 feet. In addition, the pattern plot\nshows that an rms tolerance in the order of O. 1 inch is required to maintain a random\nerror sidelobe level below the no-error pattern sidelobe level.\nSymbols used in the analysis which follows are defined below. It also includes the DAS/Shuttle Integration activities.\n1.1 PROGRAM MANAGEMENT\n\nThis element summarizes the management activities of planning, organlzmg, direct-\n\nI\n\ning, coordinating, controlling, and approving actions required to accomplish overall DAS\nobjectives. It excludes management activities associated with specific DAS hardware ele-\n\n!\n\nments but includes the management activities required for DAS integration with other\ncategories. The management activities associated with specific DAS hardware elements\nwill be included as part of those particular elements. Functions cos ted herein include\nProgram Manager, Administrative Staff, Cost/Performance Management, Information\nManagement, Contract Administration, and Material Procurement.\n\n1. 2 SYSTEM ENGINEERING AND INTEGRATION\n\n.\n\nf\nt\nt\n\nI.\n\nt!\n\n~\n\nI\n\n.\n\np\n\nThis element summarizes the effort required for systems engineering and integration\nof the DAS. The systems engineering effort includes directing and controlling requirements\n\n{\n\nanalysis and integration, system definition, system test definition, interface, safety, reli\xc2\xb7\xc2\xb7\nability, maintainability, configuration management, quality engineering and technology\nutilization. The integration effort includes DAS/Shuttle integration.\nThis element is subdivided as follows:\nWBS\n\nTITLE\n\n1. 2.1\n\nSystems Engineering\n\n1. 2.2\n\nDAS/Orbiter Integration\n\n1. 2.1 Systems Engineering\n--.~-\n\nThis element pertains to the systems engineering effort associated with the design,\ndevelopment, production and test of the DAS. Included are analyses required to verify compatibility of designs with requirements; to control and direct the engineerj,ng activities, and\nto make cost/performance tradeoffs. Also included are engineering planning studies,\n\nF-2\n\n~~q~ \xe2\x80\xa2 \xe2\x80\xa2 \xe2\x80\xa2\n\nl. ..\n\n~,\n\n........~. - ~ "--~--"\'~""\'\'\'\'\'\'\'"-"\'---\'\'\'~~\'\'\'--~~"",:",~___. Included are system analysis, test and\nevaluation required to ensure the efficient accomplishment of this task; analysis and identification of DAS test and checkout operations affecting the interface; evaluation/coordination\nof recommended changes to this interface; and preparation, submittal, and maintenance of\nInterface Control Documents. DAS integration in this context includes the deployable\nantenna in each of four configurations; two each for antenna development and mission experiment. Integration of the subsatellite and instrumentation with the orbiter is also included\nas appropriate for the different configurations.\n1.3 DESIGN AND DEVELOPMENT\nThis item includes all labor, materials, tooling, facilities, studies, analyses, etc.,\nwhich are :required to determine specification requirements and the subsequent analysis,\ndesign, development, evaluation and redesign for the SUbsystems. Specifically included are\nthe preparation of specifications, drawings, parts lists, wiring diagrams, technical coordination between engineering and manufactuling, vendor coordination, component and subsystem hardware development and testing, data reduction, report preparation, determination and specification of requirements for reliability, maintainability, and quality assurance.\nIn addition, costs are included for efforts to complete the planning and design of tooling,\njigs, fixtures, and special test equipment/GSE. Also included in this item is the effort\nexpended in conducting system design reviews, evaluating the results of those reviews, and\nperforming engineering cost analysis and materials analysis. E,\n17 June 1977.\n2.1.2 NASA HQ, "Safety Policy and Requirements for Payloads Using the Space\nTransportation System", June 1976 (updated April 1977).\n2.2 REFERENCE DOCUMEN\'rS\n2.2.1 NASA TMX-64627 "Space and Planetary Environment Criteria Guidelines for\nUse in Space Vehicle Development".\n2.2.2 Grumman Aerospace Corporation, "Deployable Antenna Demonstration Project" :E\'inal Report, Contract NAS 8-32394, (March 1978).\n3.0 DAS SHUTTLE PAYLOAD REQUIREMENTS\nThe DAS Shuttle Payload is an instrumented deployable antenna capable of being\nlaunched in the STS Orbiter.\n\nThe DAS Shuttle Payload, as defined for this specification,\n\nshall include the deployable antenna, instrumentation, RF feeds and receivers, RF source\nsatellite and Shuttle Orbiter payload bay adapters as required to meet the functional\nspeCifications state in this document.\n\nG-2\n\nQ\n\n3\n\n~~~~~~\n\n\'"\n\n.\n"-lmU"1\n\n~\'HiP\'_ Hi\n\nr\n\nAI .........."""\', __,-"._..,.\n\ni\n\nI\n\nI\n\nThe DAS flight operations program will include demonstration of the patClmging,\n\n!\n\ntransportation and deployment of a deployable antenna and the evaluation of its performance\n\n"\n\n,\n,\n\nI\n\ntI\n\nin the space environment. It is intended that the deployed antenna will remain attached to\nthe Orbiter for flight test and will be retracted into the orbiter payload bay for return to\nearth.\n\nTwo antenna configurations can be demonstrated using a single primary deployable\n\nstructure: a parabolic reflector and a flat-faced lens.\n\nEither configuration is to be capable\n\nof modification between flights to accommodate the other configuration.\nThe DAS Shuttle Payload shall be designed to operate at a nominal 555 km orbit, but\nshall be operatable at 370 to 35,600 kIn altitude. It shall remain on orbit for 7 to 30 days\nduring a given flight test, but the basic deployable antenna shall be designed for a five year\nsu:rvival in low Earth orbit to permit later modification for a free-flight mission application.\n3.1 SHUTTLE PA YLOAD FUNCTIONAL REQUIREMENTS\n3. 1. 1 Antenna Development Functions\n3.1.1. 1 Launch, Deployment and Retrieval\n\n.\n\nThe DAS Shuttle Payload shall be capable of being packaged to fit within the Orbiter\nbay for launch and transportation to orbit.\n\nThe deployable antenna shall be capable of\n\nbeing deployed and furled (retrieved while attached to the Orbiter). Deployment and\n\nI\n\n1\n\nj\'\n\nfurlment operations shall be autonomous to the maximum extent possible; minimizing the\nOrbiter and crew support requirements.\n\nCrew EVA shall not be required during these\n\noperations. In its furled configuration, the DAS Shuttle Payload shall be capable of being\nreturned to Earth for refurbishment and subsequent use on later flights.\n3.1. 1. 2 Structural Tests\nAfter deployment operations have been completed the antenna shall be capable of\nachieving the surface conformation characteristics required for antenna operation, in the\nspace environment under the influence of expected thermal and dynamic loads. Suitable\nmeasurements devices/instrumentation shall be provided to verify that structural performance requirements are satisfied.\n3. 1. 1. 3 RF Tests\nThe deployed antenna shall be capable of receiving signals from an RF source for\nmeasurements of the beam pattern. The RF source shall be sufficiently separated from\nt\n\n.r\n\nthe antenna to provide a far field measurement. Antenna pointing/slewing as required for\na pattern mapping shall be provided by the Orbiter.\n\nG-3\n\n,\'~\n\n..\n\n-~\n\n.\n\n1\n\nj\n\nI\n\n3.1. 2 Mission Experiment Functions\n3.1.2.1\n\nCommunication~\n\nExperiment\n\nFunctions to be provided in the communications experiment are TBO.\n3.1. 2.2 Radiometry Experiment\nFunctions to be provided in the radiometer experiment are TBO.\n3.2 SHUTTLE PA YLOAO PERFORMANCE REQUIREMENTS\n3.2. 1 Primary Antenna Structure\n3.2.1.1 General\nThe primary antenna structure shall be capable of supporting either of two antenna\nconfigurations: a parabolic reflector and a flat-faced lens. It shall provide feed electronics\nand a support mast with F/O as defined in subsections 3.2.2 and 3.2.3. It shall have\nprovisions for separation from the Orbiter for later use in free flight.\n3.2.1. 2 Size\nThe deployed diameter of the antenna shall be 100 meters.\n\nDimensions of the launch\n\nconfiguration (packaged) shall be consistent with Orbiter payload bay capacity as defined\nin Applicable Document 2.2.1.\n\nThese dimensions must also take into consideration the\n\nvolume requirements for a payload bay adapter, RF source subsatellite and all other flight\nsupport equipment which is mounted in the payload bay. This\nerror can vary inversely as the radius for points on the antenna approaching the center of\nthe antenna. This tolerance is equivalent to an error of A/16 for operation at 1. 4 GHz.\n3.2. 3. 3 Structure Tolerances\nAs a goal, antenna structural elements shall maintain a tolerance of an order\n3\nof magnitude better than the surface (less than 1.34 x 10- meters radial error at\nthe rim of the array) to demonstrate technology applicable to antenna frequencies\nup to 14 GHz.\n3.2.3.4 Electrical Configuration\nFor the initial flight test, the antenna shall be configured as a bootlace lens.\n\n.\n.\n\nThe\n\ndesign concept should also be applicable to eventual use in a mission experiment with\neither of two generic class of array: (1) low power illumination of active receive/transmit\nphased array lens, (3) high power illumination of passive phase-shifter lens.\n3. 2. 3. 5 Focal Length\nThe antenna feed shall be positioned to provide an F /n between 0.5 and 1.
